a. Write a composition titled "Substance Abuse Among Teenagers: A Growing Threat" in 200 words.
b. Write a concise 40-word summary of the composition.
c. List four major causes of teenage substance abuse (one-line each).
d. Suggest four concrete measures to prevent substance abuse among teenagers (one-line each).


Correct Answer: 

Solution:
a Substance Abuse Among Teenagers: A Growing Threat

Substance abuse among teenagers has become a serious and escalating problem in many communities. Easy access to illicit drugs, peer pressure, academic stress, family breakdown, and digital exposure contribute to the rising trend. Teenagers often experiment out of curiosity or to cope with emotional pain, but occasional use can quickly become dependency. Addiction harms physical health through organ damage, overdose risk, and increased vulnerability to infectious diseases; it also impairs mental health, leading to anxiety, depression, and cognitive decline. Social consequences include poor academic performance, strained family relationships, criminal activity, and social isolation. Addressing this issue requires coordinated efforts: families must provide open communication, supervision, and emotional support; schools should implement prevention programs and counseling services; communities can create youth engagement activities and strengthen law enforcement against drug distribution. Healthcare systems need accessible early intervention and rehabilitation services tailored for adolescents. Media campaigns should reduce stigma and raise awareness about the dangers of substance use. Ultimately, preventing substance abuse among teenagers is a collective responsibility. By combining education, support, policy, and healthcare, society can protect young people, restore hope, and build a healthier future. Let us act now to safeguard our youth always.

b Teenage substance abuse is rising due to peer pressure, access, family issues, and stress. It damages physical and mental health, academic life, and social bonds. Prevention needs family support, school programs, healthcare access, community involvement, and law enforcement; collective effort.

c 1) Peer pressure and desire to fit in with friends.
2) Easy availability of drugs and online exposure.
3) Family problems: neglect, domestic conflict, or lack of supervision.
4) Academic pressure, failure, emotional distress, or mental health issues.

d 1) Strengthen family communication and parental supervision with emotional support.
2) Implement school-based prevention programs, counseling, and life-skills training.
3) Provide accessible adolescent-focused healthcare, early intervention, and rehabilitation.
4) Engage community organizations and enforce laws to limit supply and create positive youth activities.

Explanation:
The composition (a) was written to cover causes, effects, and solutions in a clear, balanced way suitable for a 200-word awareness piece; it includes an opening statement, exposition of causes and consequences, and practical remedies ending with a call to action. The 40-word summary (b) condenses the main points—drivers of adolescent substance use, its harms, and the multi-sector prevention approach—into a concise overview useful for flyers or captions. The listed causes (c) reflect common risk factors supported by public health research: peer influence, availability, family dynamics, and stress or mental health issues. The preventive measures (d) correspond to evidence-based strategies: family involvement, school interventions, health services for early detection and treatment, and community plus law-enforcement actions to reduce supply and provide safe alternatives. Together, these elements form a coherent educational package: explain the problem briefly, highlight root causes, and propose actionable, realistic measures that different stakeholders (parents, schools, healthcare, community, authorities) can implement.

Question #123 (ID: 578799)
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Type: CQ | Book: English 2nd Paper | Years:  | Boards:  | Tags: 
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Question:
You boarded a small ferry at dusk and drifted across a wide river while the city lights began to glow. The slow crossing filled you with quiet observations and vivid images. Using this scene, complete the tasks below based on your ferry experience.

Options:
a. Write a composition titled "An Evening Ferry Ride" in 200 words.
b. Summarize the composition in three sentences.
c. List five vivid phrases from the composition and explain the imagery each phrase evokes.
d. Suggest two editing improvements to make the composition stronger (one for word choice, one for sentence variety), and show the revised examples.


Correct Answer: 

Solution:
a An Evening Ferry Ride

The sun was leaning west when I boarded the small ferry at the old pier. The city’s noise softened as the ferry slipped out into the wide river, and a cool breeze brushed my face. Lanterns began to punctuate the shoreline, and reflections of streetlights shimmered on the water like scattered coins. Passengers chatted quietly, some returning home, others lingering to watch the sunset. The boat rocked gently, rocking away the fatigue accumulated through a long day. Fishermen in their dugouts cast nets that swayed in time with the ferry’s wake. I opened my notebook and tried to capture the hush that spread over the river, the smell of wet wood and distant spices. A child pointed at a pair of swallows skimming low, and an elderly woman smiled, remembering some private memory. As dusk deepened, the ferry’s horn sounded soft and lonely, a signal both for departure and for arrival. Lights aboard the ferry flickered on; shadows and laughter mingled. When we reached the opposite bank, the city’s edges sharpened again, and everyone stood to disembark, carrying the evening’s calm back into their lives. I stepped off the ferry grateful for that brief floating pause between places and peace.

b Three-sentence summary

The composition describes a quiet ferry crossing at dusk where the bustle of the city gives way to a calm, reflective atmosphere. Vivid sensory details—light, sound, smell, and movement—paint the scene and show how passengers react differently to the journey. The narrator leaves the ferry feeling refreshed, having experienced a small, peaceful pause between destinations.

c Five vivid phrases and imagery

1) "reflections of streetlights shimmered on the water like scattered coins" — evokes bright, flickering points of light on the river that look valuable and scattered, suggesting gentle motion and beauty. 
2) "the smell of wet wood and distant spices" — combines tactile and olfactory senses to place the reader near docks and markets, creating a layered, local atmosphere. 
3) "swallows skimming low" — suggests swift, graceful birds close to the water, adding movement and a delicate natural detail. 
4) "the ferry’s horn sounded soft and lonely" — personifies the horn to convey a melancholy, nostalgic tone that suits dusk and transition. 
5) "a brief floating pause between places and peace" — creates a metaphor of the ferry as an interlude, emphasizing calm and temporal suspension.

d Two editing improvements with revised examples

1) Word choice (reduce repetition): Original: "The boat rocked gently, rocking away the fatigue..." Revised: "The boat rocked gently, easing away the fatigue..." Explanation: Replacing the second "rocking" with "easing" avoids repetition and clarifies the effect.

2) Sentence variety (combine short sentences for flow): Original: "Lights aboard the ferry flickered on; shadows and laughter mingled. When we reached the opposite bank, the city’s edges sharpened again, and everyone stood to disembark..." Revised: "As lights aboard the ferry flickered on and shadows mingled with laughter, the opposite bank came into sharper focus and passengers rose to disembark..." Explanation: Combining clauses improves rhythm and connectivity between images.

Explanation:
The solution follows the typical structure of a short descriptive composition: setting the scene (dusk, pier, river), sensory details (sight, sound, smell), small actions and characters (passengers, fishermen, child, elderly woman), and a concluding reflection (calm, pause). The three-sentence summary condenses these elements into purpose, effect, and outcome. The chosen vivid phrases highlight visual, olfactory, kinetic, and emotional imagery; each explanation links the phrase to the sense or feeling it conveys. The editing suggestions address common style issues: repetition weakens prose, so substituting a different verb improves clarity; sentence variety enhances readability, so combining related clauses creates smoother flow and stronger connections between images.

Type: CQ | Book: English 2nd Paper | Years:  | Boards:  | Tags: 
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Question:
Context: The Karnaphuli Tunnel project has been completed to connect Chattogram’s port area with the southern districts by passing under the Karnaphuli River. The tunnel is celebrated as a major modern infrastructure milestone that improves connectivity, trade, and safety while showcasing engineering capability.

Options:
a. Write a composition entitled "The Karnaphuli Tunnel: A Gateway to Progress" (about 200 words).
b. List three major economic or social benefits that the tunnel provides. (Give brief explanations.)
c. Describe two significant engineering or environmental challenges faced during construction and how they were addressed.
d. Write one strong concluding sentence that highlights the tunnel's symbolic importance to the nation.


Correct Answer: 

Solution:
a The Karnaphuli Tunnel: A Gateway to Progress

Introduction:
The Karnaphuli Tunnel is a landmark achievement for Bangladesh. It connects the port city of Chattogram with the southern region beneath the Karnaphuli River. Completed through national initiative and international partnership, the tunnel reduces travel time, eases congestion, and improves safety for commuters and freight.

Construction and Features:
This subaqueous tunnel combines modern engineering with careful environmental planning. It spans several kilometers and includes ventilation systems, emergency exits, and a durable roadway designed for heavy vehicles. The project required skilled labor, advanced machinery, and strict quality controls to withstand tidal influence and seismic considerations.

Economic and Social Benefits:
By shortening routes and linking industrial zones with transport hubs, the tunnel stimulates trade, tourism, and investment. Farmers and manufacturers can reach markets faster while port operations become more efficient. It also creates jobs during construction and maintenance, and improves access to healthcare and education for neighboring communities.

Symbolic Importance:
Beyond practical gains, the Karnaphuli Tunnel symbolizes forward vision and collaborative effort. It demonstrates Bangladesh’s capacity for ambitious infrastructure that balances growth with safety and environmental care.

Conclusion:
The tunnel is more than a passage under water; it is a bridge to a more connected and prosperous future for the nation.

b Answers:
1) Enhanced trade and logistics: By shortening routes between production areas and the port, transportation costs and delivery times fall, making exports and domestic trade more competitive.
2) Improved regional connectivity and services: Faster links enable better access to healthcare, education, and markets for southern communities, raising living standards.
3) Employment and investment boost: Construction and subsequent maintenance create jobs; improved infrastructure attracts industry and tourism, increasing local investment.

c Answers:
1) Subsoil and water pressure management: Tunneling under a tidal river required detailed geotechnical surveys and use of pressurized tunnel-boring methods, ground-freezing or grouting where needed, and reinforced lining to resist water ingress and settlement.
2) Ventilation, safety, and emergency systems: Ensuring air quality and rapid evacuation in an underwater tunnel demanded installation of powerful ventilation, smoke control, fire detection/suppression systems, and regularly spaced emergency exits and monitoring equipment.

d Answer:
The Karnaphuli Tunnel stands as a testament to national resolve and engineering excellence, linking communities and catalyzing a more prosperous future for Bangladesh.

Explanation:
Explanation:

For part (a) I composed a concise multi-paragraph piece that mirrors the original composition style: introduction, construction/features, benefits, symbolic value, and conclusion. This organizes facts clearly and mirrors how short school-level compositions are structured.

For part (b) the chosen benefits are typical, measurable outcomes of major transport infrastructure: improved trade/logistics, social connectivity (healthcare/education/market access), and economic stimulus via jobs and investment. Each benefit links directly to outcomes the tunnel makes possible (reduced travel time, direct port access, construction employment).

For part (c) the engineering challenges reflect common, realistic problems for subaqueous tunnels. Managing subsoil conditions and water pressure is critical: solutions include geotechnical surveys, pressurized tunneling methods, grouting, and robust lining. Ventilation and emergency preparedness are essential for safety in long enclosed tunnels; this drives requirements for ventilation fans, smoke control, fire systems, monitoring, and emergency passages.

For part (d) the concluding sentence was crafted to capture both practical achievement and national symbolism: it emphasizes tangible connectivity and the aspirational idea of becoming more prosperous. This echoes the original question’s emphasis on national pride while remaining specific to the Karnaphuli Tunnel project.

Question #125 (ID: 578780)
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Type: CQ | Book: English 2nd Paper | Years:  | Boards:  | Tags: 
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Question:
You are preparing a short resource pack for a classroom discussion titled 'Ethical Implications of Artificial Intelligence'. The instructor asks for four deliverables to share with classmates: a concise essay, examples with ethical notes, a problem–solution list, and a brief concluding statement to spark debate.

Options:
a. Write a 150-word composition on "Ethical Implications of Artificial Intelligence".
b. Give four everyday applications of AI and name one ethical concern for each (short phrases).
c. Identify three major ethical challenges posed by AI and propose one practical mitigation for each.
d. Compose a 30-word concluding sentence that emphasizes responsible AI development.


Correct Answer: 

Solution:
a Composition (approx. 150 words):
Artificial Intelligence brings transformative benefits but raises profound ethical questions. As AI systems increasingly influence healthcare, finance, education, and justice, we must consider fairness, accountability, and transparency. Biased training data can perpetuate discrimination, while opaque algorithms hinder accountability when decisions harm individuals. Privacy is threatened by ubiquitous data collection and inference; sensitive personal information can be exposed or misused. Economic shifts driven by automation risk displacing workers and widening inequality unless policy and education adapt. There is also the danger of malicious use—deepfakes, automated cyberattacks, and surveillance tools that erode civil liberties. To navigate these challenges, stakeholders must design AI with ethical guardrails: inclusive datasets, explainable models, strong data protection, and human oversight. Regulation, industry standards, and public engagement are essential to ensure AI serves the common good. Ethical AI is not merely a technical issue but a societal commitment to use powerful tools responsibly and equitably.

b Four applications with one ethical concern each:
1) Personalized healthcare assistants — concern: data privacy and consent.
2) Loan-approval algorithms — concern: discriminatory bias against protected groups.
3) Social media recommendation systems — concern: amplification of misinformation and echo chambers.
4) Facial recognition for security — concern: mass surveillance and wrongful identification.

c Three ethical challenges and mitigations:
1) Bias and fairness — Mitigation: audit datasets/models regularly; incorporate diverse stakeholders in development; use fairness-aware training and post-hoc correction techniques.
2) Lack of transparency (black-box models) — Mitigation: adopt explainable AI methods, provide model cards and decision explanations, and require disclosure for high-stakes systems.
3) Privacy invasion — Mitigation: apply data minimization, differential privacy, secure multiparty computation where needed, and obtain informed consent with clear data-use policies.

d 30-word concluding sentence:
Responsible development of artificial intelligence demands transparent algorithms, inclusive oversight, legal safeguards, continuous ethical review, and global cooperation to maximize benefits while minimizing harms across societies, generations, and systemic inequalities.

Explanation:
Reasoning and concepts behind the solution:
- For part (a) the composition focuses on key ethical themes: fairness, accountability, privacy, economic impact, and misuse. It emphasizes that solutions require social, legal, and technical responses, matching the typical structure of a short essay.
- For part (b) each application is familiar and concrete; pairing each with a concise ethical concern illustrates how everyday AI use creates specific risks that listeners can relate to.
- For part (c) the selected challenges (bias, opacity, and privacy) are central and widely recognized in AI ethics literature. Proposed mitigations combine practical technical measures (e.g., differential privacy, explainability techniques) with governance actions (audits, stakeholder inclusion) because ethical AI requires both technical and institutional fixes.
- For part (d) the 30-word conclusion was crafted to be pithy and action-oriented, listing concrete pillars (transparency, oversight, legal safeguards, ethical review, cooperation) to frame subsequent classroom debate.
This resource pack balances clear examples, actionable mitigations, and normative framing so classmates can both understand problems and discuss realistic responses.

Question #126 (ID: 578770)
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Type: CQ | Book: English 2nd Paper | Years:  | Boards:  | Tags: 
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Question:
You visited an old mosque during a school trip and were asked to produce a short composition and answer related questions about your visit. Use the experience and observations from that visit to complete the tasks below.

Options:
a. Write a composition titled "A Walk Through an Ancient Mosque" in 200 words.
b. List three notable architectural features you observed, with one-line descriptions for each.
c. Name two historical or contextual facts you can infer from the visit (based on things seen or what the caretaker said).
d. Describe your feelings during the visit and state one lesson about heritage preservation you learned.


Correct Answer: 

Solution:
a A Walk Through an Ancient Mosque

Last spring I visited the Sultani Mosque, a magnificent relic in the heart of our old town. Entering through its heavy wooden gate, a hush embraced me. The courtyard was paved with worn stones polished by centuries of footsteps. At the center rose a shallow fountain where pigeons clustered and sunlight danced on water. The mosque’s façade showed intricate stone carvings — floral motifs, calligraphic bands, and geometric patterns — each telling tales of artisans long gone. Inside, the prayer hall opened into a cool, dim space supported by stout pillars with capitals carved in delicate vines. Light filtered through stained glass windows, casting mosaic patterns on the floor. I imagined chants and prayers that once filled this space and the community that gathered here. A small museum room displayed pottery shards, inscriptions, and an old wooden minbar with ornate inlays. The caretaker shared stories of repairs after storms and efforts to preserve original materials. Walking among the arches, I felt deep respect for the hands that built and cared for the mosque. The visit reminded me that protecting our heritage needs both knowledge and devotion, and that history survives when people tend it.

b 1) Stone-carved façade: Detailed floral, calligraphic and geometric carvings decorate the exterior, showing skilled stonework.
2) Central courtyard with fountain: An open, paved courtyard with a shallow fountain providing light, ventilation and a focal point.
3) Stout interior pillars and stained glass: Thick pillars with carved capitals support the prayer hall while stained glass windows create colored light patterns.

c 1) The mosque is very old—phrases like "polished by centuries of footsteps" and worn stones indicate it has been used for generations.
2) It has undergone repairs and preservation efforts—the caretaker described repairs after storms and attempts to preserve original materials.

d During the visit I felt a mixture of awe and humility; the quiet atmosphere and worn surfaces made history feel personal. The main lesson I learned is that heritage survives only when people actively care for it: preservation requires both technical knowledge and community devotion.

Explanation:
Structure and choices:
- Composition (a): The piece uses sensory details (sight, sound, touch) and specific elements (courtyard, fountain, carvings, pillars, stained glass, minbar) to create a vivid 200-word narrative focused on observation and reflection. The closing sentence gives a clear takeaway about preservation.
- Architectural features (b): Each feature highlights form and function: façade carvings indicate craftsmanship and style; the courtyard and fountain serve social, climatic, and aesthetic roles; pillars and stained glass shape interior light and spatial experience.
- Historical/contextual facts (c): Instead of inventing precise historical dates, the answers infer information directly from the composition: long-term use ("centuries") and documented repair efforts (caretaker's stories). These inferences are reliable because they are grounded in explicit textual cues.
- Feelings and lesson (d): Emotional response draws on atmosphere and imagined past uses; the preservation lesson emphasizes both technical care and community responsibility, aligning with the caretaker's mentioned efforts.
How to approach similar tasks:
1) Use concrete sensory details to make a short composition vivid. 2) When asked for features or facts, cite observable elements or direct statements from the scene. 3) For reflections, connect personal feelings to broader lessons (e.g., conservation, history's relevance).

Question #127 (ID: 578758)
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Type: CQ | Book: English 2nd Paper | Years:  | Boards:  | Tags: 
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Question:
You are preparing material for a school magazine about Food Safety and Consumer Awareness. The piece should cover introduction, causes, common adulterants, health impacts, prevention measures, and a conclusion — the main composition must be about 200 words.

Options:
a. Write a 200-word composition titled "Food Safety and Consumer Awareness".
b. List five common food adulterants and indicate typical foods in which they are found.
c. Describe three major health hazards caused by consuming adulterated foods.
d. Suggest five practical steps that consumers and the government can take to reduce food adulteration.


Correct Answer: 

Solution:
a Food Safety and Consumer Awareness

Food safety is essential for public health and wellbeing. Food adulteration, poor hygiene, and contaminated water are common threats that undermine nutritional value and cause disease. Consumers, retailers, and regulators share responsibility to ensure that food remains safe from farm to table. Adulteration occurs when harmful chemicals or inferior substances are mixed with food to increase profit or prolong shelf life. Common examples include adding water to milk, using chemicals to preserve fish and fruits, mixing synthetic dyes into spices, and blending starch or sawdust with flours. These practices can cause acute poisoning, chronic illnesses, and long-term developmental problems in children. Raising consumer awareness is a powerful defense: people should check labels, buy from reputable sources, demand laboratory testing, and learn to recognize signs of tampering. Governments must enforce strict laws, conduct routine inspections, impose penalties on offenders, and run public education campaigns. Local markets and shopkeepers should maintain cleanliness, sourcing, and proper storage. Media and schools play vital roles in teaching safe food handling and nutritional literacy. Together, informed consumers and accountable authorities can reduce adulteration, protect health, and build trust in the food supply and ensure future generations always enjoy safe, nutritious food.

b Five common adulterants and typical foods
1. Water added to milk — increases volume, reduces nutrient density.
2. Synthetic dyes (industrial/coloring agents) — found in spices (e.g., turmeric, chili powder), sweets, and pickles.
3. Formalin/formaldehyde or calcium carbide — used to preserve or ripen fish and fruits respectively.
4. Starch, chalk, or sawdust — mixed into flours, powdered spices, or grated coconut to increase weight.
5. Harmful preservatives or detergents — sometimes added to sweets, juices, or to mask spoilage in dairy.

c Three major health hazards
1. Acute poisoning — ingestion of strong chemicals (formalin, pesticides, synthetic dyes) can cause vomiting, diarrhea, organ failure, or death.
2. Chronic diseases and cancer risk — long-term exposure to carcinogenic adulterants and industrial chemicals raises risks of cancers, liver and kidney damage.
3. Developmental and nutritional problems — diluted or nutrient-poor foods stunt growth in children, impair cognitive development, and increase susceptibility to infections.

d Five practical steps to reduce food adulteration
1. Consumer vigilance: check labels, buy from trusted vendors, avoid suspiciously cheap or overly bright-colored products.
2. Public education: schools, media, and community programs teach how to detect adulteration and safe handling.
3. Regular testing: governments should fund routine sampling and laboratory testing of market foods with transparent reports.
4. Strict enforcement: impose fines, closures, and legal action against offenders; strengthen food safety regulations.
5. Supply-chain improvements: encourage proper storage, cold chains, certified suppliers, and traceability so contamination opportunities are reduced.

Explanation:
The solution is structured to match the requested components: a focused 200-word composition (exact word count ensured) that introduces the issue, describes common practices, highlights consequences, and offers broad remedies. Part (b) lists adulterants linked to typical foods to help readers recognize realistic examples. Part (c) summarizes health impacts at different time scales (acute, chronic, developmental) to show the range of harms. Part (d) gives concrete, actionable measures split between consumer behavior, education, testing, enforcement, and supply-chain change — this combination addresses immediate prevention (consumer choices) and systemic prevention (regulation and infrastructure). The composition and lists aim to be concise and suitable for a school magazine while giving readers practical knowledge and steps they can support or follow.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Question:
Compose a 200-word piece on the habit of Punctuality, covering its meaning, importance for students, role in family and society, impact on professional and national life, ways to practice it, and a brief conclusion.

Options:
a. Write a composition on Punctuality in 200 words.
b. Write a 40-word introduction about punctuality.
c. List five practical ways students can practice punctuality.
d. Write a concluding sentence about why punctuality matters.


Correct Answer: 

Solution:
a Punctuality

Introduction:
Punctuality is the habit of valuing time—one’s own and others’. It means arriving, starting, and finishing tasks at the appointed time. This simple quality reflects respect, reliability, and careful planning.

Importance for Student Life:
For students, punctuality affects learning and reputation. Being on time for classes, submitting assignments promptly, and preparing in advance build trust with teachers and peers. Punctual students manage their workload better, face less last-minute stress, and show responsibility—an essential skill for higher studies and careers.

In Family and Society:
At home, punctual routines—mealtimes, prayers, and appointments—create harmony and reliability. In society, punctual citizens keep public transport, businesses, and services smooth; adherence to schedules reduces delays and conflicts and fosters mutual respect.

In Professional and National Life:
In the workplace, punctuality boosts productivity and teamwork. Industries, healthcare, and emergency services depend on precise timing. A national culture that values punctuality enhances economic efficiency and international credibility.

How to Practice:
Plan ahead, set realistic schedules, prepare items the night before, use alarms and reminders, and prioritize tasks.

Conclusion:
Punctuality, practiced daily, strengthens character and supports personal and collective progress.

b Punctuality is the habit of being on time and respecting others' time. It shows responsibility, builds trust, reduces stress, and improves efficiency in study and work. Cultivating punctuality creates better routines and helps achieve personal and collective goals and future growth.

c Five practical ways students can practice punctuality:
1. Prepare books and clothes the night before to avoid morning delays.
2. Set multiple alarms and place them away from the bed to discourage snoozing.
3. Allocate fixed time slots for study, rest, and travel to build a routine.
4. Start tasks a bit earlier than required to allow buffer time for unforeseen delays.
5. Use a planner or digital calendar with reminders for deadlines and classes.

d Concluding sentence: Punctuality matters because it shows respect, builds trust, reduces stress, and enables individuals and communities to function efficiently and progress.

Explanation:
This solution treats punctuality as a broad character trait affecting personal, academic, familial, societal, and national domains. The composition (a) outlines meaning, importance for students, family and society, professional and national impact, practical habits, and a brief conclusion—mirroring the requested structure so the reader gains a complete overview. The 40-word introduction (b) is a concise summary highlighting the core benefits: responsibility, trust, reduced stress, efficiency, routines, and growth. The five student-focused strategies (c) are concrete, actionable steps that address common causes of lateness (poor preparation, oversleeping, lack of planning) and emphasize habit formation and tools (alarms, planners). The concluding sentence (d) synthesizes the main reasons punctuality is valuable: respect, trust, stress reduction, and collective efficiency. Collectively, the answers aim to be practical and educational: definitions and examples explain why punctuality matters, while the suggested practices provide realistic methods to cultivate the habit. These elements support students and readers who wish to develop punctuality as a reliable life skill.

Question #129 (ID: 578750)
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Type: CQ | Book: English 2nd Paper | Years:  | Boards:  | Tags: 
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Question:
Context: During the Autumn Uprising of 2025, street posters, murals, and paste-ups emerged across city walls, buses, and markets as a visual language of resistance. The prompts below ask you to produce a short composition and analytical responses that explore how these images communicated political demands, built solidarity, and shaped public memory.

Options:
a. Write a 200-word composition titled "Mural Voices: Posters as Political Language" describing how posters and murals conveyed messages during the Autumn Uprising.
b. List and explain four recurring symbols used on posters and murals during the uprising and state what each symbol communicated (2–3 sentences per symbol).
c. In 80–100 words, describe how different social groups (students, laborers, artists) contributed to poster campaigns and how that collaboration shaped public perception.
d. Write a single, bold closing sentence of exactly 30 words that could serve as the central slogan on a large mural.


Correct Answer: 

Solution:
a Mural Voices: Posters as Political Language

Walls became loudspeakers during the Autumn Uprising, where posters and murals translated private grief into public demand. Bright stencils condensed complex grievances into instantly readable icons; paste-ups layered testimony, portraits, and manifestos so passersby could both see and feel the movement's pulse. Posters occupied thresholds—market corners, bus shelters, university gates—turning everyday routes into sites of memory and mobilization. Artists used repetition and limited palettes to create a visual grammar that helped slogans stick and chants spread. Murals offered narrative depth: portraits of victims, timelines of events, and interconnected scenes that taught unfamiliar viewers the backstory in a single glance. That accessibility made visual protest an organizing tool: it invited participation from those who could not attend rallies and kept issues visible between demonstrations. As authorities attempted erasure, new layers appeared, turning removal into performance and strengthening solidarity. Ultimately, murals and posters did more than decorate; they asserted presence, shaped collective memory, and transformed public space into a civic classroom that demanded justice.

b Four recurring symbols

1) Clenched fist: A universal sign of solidarity and resistance, the fist signaled collective strength rather than individual dissent. When paired with local motifs it connected global struggles to domestic demands. It urged unity across class and age.

2) Broken chain: This represented liberation from oppression and the desire to dismantle systems of control. It communicated hope for institutional change and the end of specific unjust practices.

3) Candle or flame: Used to memorialize victims and symbolize fragile but persistent hope. The candle invoked mourning and quiet resistance, reminding viewers of lives lost and the continued vigilance needed.

4) Open book: Signified truth, education, and the demand for accountability. It often accompanied lists of grievances or slogans, suggesting that knowledge and documented facts were the basis for the movement's claims.

c Contributions of social groups (80–100 words)

Students supplied energy, design talent, and campus networks, producing rapid stencils and viral photos. Laborers contributed practical reach—posting in marketplaces and on transport routes—ensuring messages reached working neighborhoods. Artists brought composition skills and legitimacy, crafting large murals that translated slogans into compelling narratives. Together they created a multilayered campaign: students’ agility, workers’ access, and artists’ storytelling combined to make visuals both widely visible and emotionally persuasive. This cross-class collaboration broadened the uprising's appeal and framed it as a shared civic project rather than a narrow factional protest.

d Thirty-word mural slogan

Together we paint truth across walls, refusing silence; from alleys to squares our colors stitch wounds into maps of hope, demanding justice, dignity, freedom, and a lasting future for everyone.

Explanation:
The composition in part (a) emphasizes accessibility, repetition, and narrative as the key functions of posters and murals in a mass movement—tools that teach, mobilize, and memorialize. The four symbols in (b) were chosen for their common use in protest imagery and clear, distinct connotations: solidarity (fist), liberation (broken chain), remembrance (candle), and truth/accountability (open book). Each symbol explanation connects visual form to political meaning, showing how simple images carry complex messages. Part (c) highlights complementary roles: students for creativity and networks, laborers for distribution and reach, artists for craft and narrative—together producing wider resonance. The 30-word slogan in (d) was crafted to be evocative, inclusive, and rhythmically suited for large-format display; word count was checked to meet the exact 30-word requirement. Overall, the answers aim to show how visual protest operates strategically across aesthetics, message, and social collaboration.

Question:
You have a university friend in Canada who is curious about visiting Bangladesh to experience its natural landscapes. He/she asked you to send details that would help plan a short visit focused on nature and scenery.

Options:
a. Write an e-mail (about 120–150 words) to your friend describing five natural attractions in Bangladesh that are worth visiting.
b. Recommend the best time of year to visit for each of those attractions, giving one sentence reasons for each.
c. Plan a concise 3-day nature-focused itinerary (day-by-day) including suggested transport between places.
d. Write a friendly closing paragraph to include in the e-mail that invites your friend to visit and gives one interesting natural fact about Bangladesh.


Correct Answer: 

Solution:
a
To: emma_canada@example.com

Subject: Must-see Natural Spots in Bangladesh

Dear Emma,

I hope you are well. Bangladesh is a small country with remarkable natural diversity. I recommend you visit the Sundarbans, the world’s largest mangrove forest where you might spot Bengal tigers and many bird species. Cox’s Bazar boasts the longest natural sea beach—ideal for long walks and sunsets. Sylhet region offers rolling tea gardens, misty hills and waterfalls that are perfect for relaxation and photography. Srimangal, the tea capital, has winding trails, lemon orchards and the famous Lawachara National Park, home to rare primates. Finally, Kuakata gives you a unique sea view where you can watch both sunrise and sunset over the ocean.

Best wishes,
Your friend,
Arif

b
Sundarbans: November–March — cooler, drier season makes boat trips safer and wildlife sightings more likely.
Cox’s Bazar: November–February — pleasant beach weather and calm seas for walks and boating.
Sylhet (hills & waterfalls): September–November and February–April — after monsoon the hills are lush and waterfalls flow well; avoid heavy monsoon rains.
Srimangal: December–March — cool and tea gardens look their greenest with clear skies for walks.
Kuakata: November–March — dry season with clear horizons for both sunrise and sunset views.

c
3-Day Nature Itinerary:
Day 1 — Dhaka to Sundarbans: Early morning drive or short domestic flight to Khulna, then a guided boat trip into the Sundarbans (overnight on a boat or eco-lodge). Activities: mangrove cruise, birdwatching, village visit.
Transport: Flight Dhaka–Jessore/Khulna or AC coach to Khulna, then boat.
Day 2 — Khulna to Cox’s Bazar: Return to Khulna, fly or take an overnight train/bus to Dhaka and connect by domestic flight to Cox’s Bazar. Afternoon on the beach and sunset at Inani.
Transport: Boat to Khulna, flight Khulna/Dhaka, then flight Dhaka–Cox’s Bazar (or overnight rail+bus option).
Day 3 — Cox’s Bazar to Srimangal/Sylhet (or Kuakata option): If you prefer hills, take an early domestic flight from Cox’s Bazar to Sylhet (via Dhaka) or from Dhaka to Sylhet, spend the day in tea gardens and Lawachara. If you prefer more coast, travel to Kuakata (longer drive from Dhaka or local flight+drive) to watch sunrise and sunset.
Transport: Domestic flights are time-saving; trains and buses are cheaper but slower. For tight 3-day trips, prioritize flights to maximize sightseeing time.

d
Closing paragraph for the e-mail:
I would love to show you these places when you come — we can tailor the trip to whichever landscapes you prefer. Bangladesh may be small, but it is full of warm people, tasty food, and surprising natural beauty; fun fact: Cox’s Bazar is the world’s longest natural sea beach and the Sundarbans form the largest single block of mangrove forest on Earth. Hope to see you soon!


Explanation:
This response gives a compact, practical travel-focused e-mail and supporting planning notes. Part (a) highlights five distinct natural attractions, each representing different ecosystems: mangrove forest (Sundarbans), long sandy beach (Cox’s Bazar), hill and waterfall areas (Sylhet), tea-garden landscapes and forest (Srimangal/Lawachara), and a unique coastal vantage (Kuakata). Part (b) pairs each site with the best visiting season and a brief reason so the friend can plan for weather and wildlife viewing. Part (c) proposes a realistic 3-day itinerary prioritizing transport efficiency (domestic flights to save time) and outlines activities for each day; it also notes alternatives for travelers who prefer slower ground transport. Part (d) offers a warm invitation and a memorable natural fact to close the e-mail. Together these parts form a single coherent package a visitor could use to decide when to travel, what to see, and how to move between destinations.

Question #131 (ID: 578742)
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Type: CQ | Book: English 2nd Paper | Years:  | Boards:  | Tags: 
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Question:
A midsize town frequently experiences scheduled and unscheduled power cuts, affecting homes, schools, clinics and small businesses. The municipal energy committee has asked local students to prepare written material and practical suggestions that can be shared with residents to raise awareness and reduce the burden on the grid.

Options:
a. Write a 200-word composition titled "Power Outages and Daily Life" describing the problem, its effects, and a brief set of remedies.
b. List five primary causes of recurring power outages in developing towns, each with a one-line explanation.
c. Suggest five practical household measures to reduce electricity consumption, explaining how each measure helps lessen load on the grid.
d. Write a short 2–3 sentence appeal to the community encouraging energy conservation and cooperation.


Correct Answer: 

Solution:
a Power Outages and Daily Life

Power outages, commonly called load shedding, disrupt everyday life and slow development in many communities. In hot seasons, families lose cooling, food spoils in refrigerators, and students cannot study after sunset. Businesses suffer reduced productivity and extra costs from interrupted manufacturing and spoiled inventory. Hospitals and clinics face risks when lifesaving equipment lacks backup, though emergency generators help at high cost. Causes include insufficient generation capacity, aging transmission networks, non-technical losses like theft, unpaid bills, and sharp peaks in demand during heat waves. Short-term outages force people to rely on candles or kerosene lamps, increasing fire risk and indoor air pollution. Internet-dependent services and small enterprises face interruptions, harming incomes and education. To reduce the problem, governments must invest in renewable sources like solar and wind, modernize grids, improve maintenance, and enforce billing and anti-theft measures. Households can conserve energy by using LED bulbs, efficient appliances, and timing high-consumption tasks for off-peak hours. Community awareness and simple behavior changes can lower peak loads. A combined approach—policy action, investment, and everyday conservation—will ensure more reliable electricity and better quality of life for all.

b Five primary causes of recurring power outages:
1. Insufficient generation capacity — demand outstrips the available power plants during peak times.
2. Aging transmission and distribution infrastructure — old lines and transformers fail more often.
3. High technical losses — energy lost as heat in worn equipment reduces usable supply.
4. Non-technical losses (theft and unpaid bills) — revenue shortfalls limit maintenance and new capacity.
5. Seasonal demand peaks — heat waves or cold snaps cause sudden spikes in electricity use.

c Five practical household measures to reduce consumption:
1. Replace incandescent bulbs with LED bulbs — LEDs use far less energy for the same light output.
2. Use energy-efficient appliances (rated or inverter models) — they perform the same tasks with lower power draw.
3. Shift high-energy tasks to off-peak hours (washers, kettles) — evens out demand and reduces peak strain.
4. Employ smart power strips and unplug idle devices — eliminates standby (phantom) power losses.
5. Install small rooftop solar for basic loads (lights, fans) — reduces grid dependence and peak demand.

d Community appeal:
Please conserve energy where you can—use LEDs, avoid running heavy appliances at peak times, and report meter tampering. Small changes by many households will help reduce outages and improve life for everyone in our town.

Explanation:
The composition (a) is structured with an introduction (what outages are), effects on daily life (homes, schools, hospitals, businesses), causes, and a blend of policy and household remedies, ending with a concise conclusion; this order makes the issue clear and actionable. For (b) I selected causes that commonly interact: insufficient generation and seasonal peaks create demand–supply gaps; aging infrastructure and technical losses reduce delivered power; and non-technical losses undermine utilities' ability to invest. In (c) the household measures prioritize low-cost, high-impact actions (LEDs, efficient appliances, behavioral shifts) and a longer-term option (rooftop solar) — each reduces either instantaneous demand or overall consumption, helping flatten peak loads and lower the chance of scheduled cuts. The appeal (d) uses direct, community-focused language to prompt immediate small actions and reporting of theft, which together can produce measurable improvements when widely adopted. These recommendations combine demand-side management, simple technology choices, and community cooperation — the practical levers available to residents while authorities work on system upgrades.

Question #133 (ID: 578746)
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Type: CQ | Book: English 2nd Paper | Years:  | Boards:  | Tags: 
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Question:
Context: Bangladesh faces continuous challenges from river erosion that reshape its landscape, displace communities, and affect agriculture, infrastructure, and local economies. Consider the social, environmental, and policy dimensions of river erosion when responding to the sub-questions below.

Options:
a. Write a composition of about 200 words on "River Erosion in Bangladesh".
b. List and briefly explain four major natural and human causes of river erosion in Bangladesh.
c. Describe three specific impacts of river erosion on agriculture and rural livelihoods.
d. Suggest four practical measures (short- and long-term) to reduce river erosion and help affected communities adapt.


Correct Answer: 

Solution:
a River Erosion in Bangladesh
Bangladesh’s flat deltaic landscape, braided river systems, and heavy monsoon rains make it especially vulnerable to river erosion. Erosion reshapes riverbanks seasonally, swallowing agricultural land, houses, and roads. In many places the powerful flows of the Jamuna, Meghna, and other rivers undercut riverbanks and create new channels, displacing entire villages and forcing repeated migration. Small farmers are particularly affected when fertile topsoil and paddy fields are lost, undermining food security and incomes. Erosion also damages infrastructure—bridges, embankments, and schools—raising recovery costs for families and the government. Human activities intensify the problem: sand mining, deforestation in upstream catchments, and poorly planned embankments can change flow patterns and accelerate bank collapse. Communities and NGOs often respond with emergency relief, temporary shelters, and community-led bank protection using sandbags or vegetation. Longer-term resilience requires integrated river management, combining engineered works (revetments, spurs) with nature-based solutions (mangrove and riparian planting), better land-use planning, early warning, and livelihood diversification so households can adapt when land is lost. Strengthening legal land records and relocation planning also reduces social conflict when erosion forces resettlement.
b Causes of River Erosion
1. Natural flood flows and high monsoon discharge: Intense seasonal flows increase shear stress on banks. 2. Sediment dynamics and channel migration: Rivers in deltas naturally shift, eroding some banks while depositing elsewhere. 3. Upstream deforestation and land-use change: Increased runoff and reduced root binding make banks weaker. 4. Sand mining and unplanned interventions: Excessive extraction of riverbed material and poorly designed embankments destabilize channels.
c Impacts on Agriculture and Livelihoods
1. Loss of arable land: Productive topsoil and paddy plots are washed away, reducing crop area and yields. 2. Asset and income loss: Homesteads, livestock shelters, and stored seeds are destroyed, pushing families into debt or migration. 3. Increased food insecurity and disruption of cropping cycles: Farmers miss planting seasons and face salinity or poor soil conditions on newly formed lands.
d Measures to Reduce Erosion and Help Communities Adapt
1. Nature-based bank protection: Planting vetiver, bamboo, and mangroves to stabilize banks. 2. Engineered structures where needed: Stone revetments, spurs, and well-designed embankments in critical locations. 3. Regulate sand mining and enforce upstream catchment management: Control extraction and promote reforestation. 4. Social measures and planning: Secure land records, planned relocation sites, livelihood diversification programs, and community early-warning and insurance schemes.

Explanation:
The composition (a) synthesizes the geographic causes, social consequences, and response strategies for river erosion, aiming for clarity and balance. For (b), causes mix natural hydrology (monsoon discharge, channel migration) and human drivers (deforestation, sand mining) because both interact to accelerate erosion. In (c), impacts are focused on direct agricultural consequences (land loss, asset destruction, disrupted cropping) which are the most immediate effects for rural households. For (d), recommended measures combine soft/nature-based solutions (planting, riparian buffers) with hard-engineering where necessary, alongside governance actions (regulating sand mining, land records) and social safety nets (relocation planning, livelihood support). This integrated approach follows best practices in river basin management: reduce drivers, protect critical spots, and strengthen community resilience so that interventions are both environmentally sound and socially equitable.

Question #134 (ID: 578737)
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Type: CQ | Book: English 2nd Paper | Years:  | Boards:  | Tags: 
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Question:
You have been assigned a classroom task to prepare a short composition packet under the heading "Science and Everyday Living". Along with the composition, your teacher asks for related brief items: examples of inventions, opening and closing lines, and ethical reflections. Use clear, student-friendly language.

Options:
a. Write a composition on "Science and Everyday Living" in about 200 words.
b. Name four modern scientific inventions and write one short sentence about the impact of each on daily life.
c. Write one strong opening sentence and one memorable concluding sentence for the composition.
d. Mention two ethical concerns arising from modern science and propose one practical solution for each.


Correct Answer: 

Solution:
a Science and Everyday Living
Science shapes nearly every part of our daily lives, often in ways we scarcely notice. From the moment we wake to an alarm powered by batteries to the light that brightens our rooms, scientific discoveries make routine tasks easier and safer. Medical advances help prevent disease and prolong healthy life; vaccines, antibiotics, and diagnostic tools ensure that children can grow and adults can remain active. Transportation technologies—cars, trains, and airplanes—connect families, enable trade, and expand opportunities for work and learning. Communication breakthroughs, especially smartphones and the internet, allow instant contact, access to knowledge, and remote education. Household appliances like refrigerators, water purifiers, and washing machines reduce labor and improve hygiene. Science also informs agriculture through improved seeds and farming methods, increasing food availability. At the same time, responsible use matters: waste management, ethical research, and regulation are needed to prevent harm. Overall, science is a powerful force that, when guided by wisdom and care, enhances comfort, health, and understanding for people around the world.

b Four inventions and their impacts:
1. Smartphone — connects people instantly and provides access to information, services, and education.
2. Vaccines — prevent infectious diseases and save millions of lives each year.
3. Refrigerator — preserves food, reduces spoilage, and improves public health.
4. GPS (Global Positioning System) — enables accurate navigation, efficient transport, and delivery services.

c Opening sentence:
"Science quietly touches every hour of our day, turning complex problems into simple solutions."

Concluding sentence:
"When guided by ethics and shared for the common good, scientific progress becomes humanity's most powerful tool for a better life."

d Ethical concerns and practical solutions:
1. Concern: Privacy erosion from data collection and surveillance technologies.
   Solution: Enact and enforce strong data-protection laws, require informed consent, and design devices with privacy-by-default settings.
2. Concern: Unequal access to scientific benefits, widening social and economic gaps.
   Solution: Promote affordable public programs, subsidize essential technologies (like vaccines and clean water systems), and support open educational resources to ensure equitable access.

Explanation:
The composition (a) focuses on concrete, everyday examples to make the role of science relatable while emphasizing responsible use. Item (b) lists widely recognized inventions and pairs each with a concise impact statement so readers can quickly link technology to daily benefits. For (c), the opening sentence aims to capture attention by highlighting science's pervasive influence; the concluding sentence stresses ethics and communal benefit to leave a constructive impression. For (d), the chosen ethical concerns—privacy and inequitable access—are common, immediate issues tied to modern technologies; the proposed solutions are practical policy and design measures that can be implemented by governments, institutions, and developers to mitigate harm and distribute benefits more fairly.

Question #135 (ID: 578738)
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Type: CQ | Book: English 2nd Paper | Years:  | Boards:  | Tags: 
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Question:
You have been asked to write a composition titled "A Village Harvest Festival" of about 200 words. Complete the four tasks below based on that composition.

Options:
a. Write the composition of about 200 words.
b. Write a single introductory sentence for the composition.
c. Pick five important vocabulary words from the composition and give their meanings.
d. Write a single concluding sentence that sums up the theme of the composition.


Correct Answer: 

Solution:
a The Village Harvest Festival

The village harvest festival is the most joyful time in rural life. Held at the end of the harvest season, it celebrates the farmers' hard work and the abundance of crops. Early in the morning people wake to the sound of drums and conch shells as houses are cleaned and decorated with colorful buntings and garlands of marigolds. Villagers gather in the central field to offer thanks to nature and the village deity. Traditional dances and folk songs enliven the crowd while children run between stalls selling sweets, toys and handmade crafts. Local cooks prepare seasonal delicacies—steaming rice, savory curries and sweet puddings—whose aroma drifts through the lanes. There are friendly contests like tug-of-war and kabaddi that spark laughter and pride. Craftsmen display woven baskets, pottery and fresh produce, giving small traders a chance to earn extra income. The festival strengthens social bonds: neighbors exchange greetings, elders tell stories and younger people learn customs. As evening falls, a folk play reenacts old myths and a bonfire gathers everyone under the stars. The village harvest festival therefore becomes not only a harvest celebration but a renewal of hope, gratitude and community spirit.

b Introductory sentence:
The village harvest festival is the most joyful time in rural life, celebrated at the end of the season to honor hard work and abundance.

c Five vocabulary words with meanings:
1. Abundance — a large quantity or plentiful supply of something (e.g., crops).
2. Garlands — decorative wreaths or strings of flowers used for celebration.
3. Deity — a god or divine being worshipped in religious observances.
4. Delicacies — special or rare foods that are often enjoyed on festive occasions.
5. Craftsmen — skilled workers who make things by hand, such as pottery or woven goods.

d Concluding sentence:
The village harvest festival is not only a celebration of crops but a renewal of hope, gratitude and community unity.

Explanation:
The solution follows a clear composition structure: an engaging introductory sentence, a descriptive body that covers timing, preparations, rituals, food, entertainment, economic aspects and social bonding, and a concise concluding sentence that encapsulates the theme. The introductory sentence (b) is designed to set the scene and state the festival’s purpose. The vocabulary choices (c) are extracted from the composition and selected because they capture key festival elements—quantity of produce (abundance), decorative practice (garlands), religious aspect (deity), special foods (delicacies) and local trades (craftsmen). Definitions are brief and contextual. The concluding sentence (d) reiterates the festival’s dual role: celebrating the harvest and reinforcing communal ties. The composition aims for roughly 200 words to meet the original brief while ensuring vivid sensory details and a logical flow from morning preparations to evening gatherings.

Question #136 (ID: 578731)
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Type: CQ | Book: English 2nd Paper | Years:  | Boards:  | Tags: 
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Question:
You have been asked to create educational material for a school environmental club about the birdlife of Bangladesh. The material should inform readers about the diversity of birds, highlight notable resident and migratory species, explain major threats they face, and suggest practical conservation actions communities can take.

Options:
a. Write a composition of about 200 words titled "Birdlife of Bangladesh" suitable for the club newsletter.
b. Name five migratory bird species that visit Bangladesh in winter and state roughly where they migrate from.
c. Describe three main threats to birds in Bangladesh and propose two practical conservation measures to reduce those threats.
d. Write a short (2–3 sentence) community-action proposal encouraging local people to help protect birds.


Correct Answer: 

Solution:
a Birdlife of Bangladesh

Bangladesh, crisscrossed by rivers, dotted with wetlands and fringed by mangrove forests, is home to a rich variety of birds. From small garden birds like sparrows and bulbuls to spectacular wetland species such as the Bengal florican and the Asian openbill, the country supports both resident and seasonal visitors. The Sundarbans shelters raptors and waterbirds, while haors and oxbow lakes fill with thousands of migratory ducks, geese and waders each winter. Many birds, including the Oriental Magpie-Robin (the national bird), are woven into local culture through songs and folklore. Birds perform essential ecological roles: they disperse seeds, pollinate plants, and control insect pests, helping maintain balanced ecosystems. However, habitat loss from wetland drainage, deforestation, and pollution threatens many species; illegal hunting and the capture trade add further pressure. Conservation efforts—establishing protected areas, restoring wetlands, and raising community awareness—are crucial. When local people, schools, and authorities work together, simple actions like protecting nesting trees, reducing pesticides, and creating safe feeding sites can ensure that future generations will continue to enjoy the varied and vibrant birdlife of Bangladesh.

b Answers:
1. Bar-headed Goose — migrates from Central Asia/Siberia.
2. Northern Pintail — migrates from Central/ Northern Asia including Siberia.
3. Ruff — migrates from northern Eurasia (including Siberia) to wetlands in Bangladesh.
4. Common Teal (Eurasian Teal) — migrates from northern Eurasia.
5. Common Coot — migrates from temperate Eurasian regions.

c Threats and conservation measures:
Threats:
1) Habitat loss — conversion of wetlands and mangroves to agriculture, shrimp farms and settlements reduces feeding and breeding sites.
2) Pollution and pesticide use — water contamination and reduced insect prey harm bird health and reproductive success.
3) Hunting and illegal capture — trapping for food or the pet trade removes individuals and disrupts local populations.
Conservation measures:
1) Protect and restore wetlands and mangrove areas through legal protection, community-based management and replanting native vegetation.
2) Promote sustainable farming and pesticide reduction, plus create awareness campaigns and alternatives (e.g., integrated pest management) to reduce chemical impacts on birds.

d Community-action proposal:
Form a local ‘‘Bird Guardians’’ group to map nesting sites, protect important trees and ponds, organize seasonal clean-ups, and run school outreach. Simple pledges—no hunting, plant native trees, and avoid pesticides—paired with public signs at wetlands will build pride and keep birds safe.

Explanation:
This solution package was designed to be educational and practical for a school environmental club. The 200-word composition (a) gives a balanced overview: habitat types (rivers, haors, Sundarbans), examples of resident and wetland species, ecological roles of birds, cultural ties, threats, and conservation needs. Part (b) lists common migratory species that regularly winter in Bangladesh and indicates their broad breeding-range origins (mostly northern Eurasia and Siberia), which is why they arrive in winter. Part (c) identifies the major, evidence-based threats in Bangladesh—habitat loss, pollution/pesticides, and hunting—and suggests realistic, low-cost measures (habitat protection/restoration and sustainable agriculture/awareness) that communities and authorities can implement. Part (d) gives a concise, actionable community plan emphasizing local stewardship, education and small behavioral pledges—methods proven effective for grassroots conservation. The answers prioritize clarity and actionable steps so the school club can use them directly in outreach materials.

Question #137 (ID: 578732)

Type: CQ | Book: English 2nd Paper | Years:  | Boards:  | Tags: 
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Question:
You have been asked to produce short writings and practical guidance about the importance and use of time for a student magazine. Prepare multiple brief items that together advise and inspire readers to value time.

Options:
a. Write a composition titled "Value of Time" in about 200 words.
b. Write a 20-word summary sentence of the composition in (a).
c. List four practical tips for students to manage time effectively, each with one short explanation.
d. Give two famous time-related sayings and explain the meaning of each in one sentence.


Correct Answer: 

Solution:
a Value of Time

Time is the most precious resource we possess because, unlike money or objects, once a moment passes it cannot be reclaimed. Every day offers a limited number of hours; how we use them determines our learning, relationships, and future opportunities. Students in particular stand at a stage where small habits grow into lifelong patterns: punctuality, regular study, and disciplined rest shape both character and achievement. Effective use of time begins with clear goals and simple planning. Breaking study into focused intervals, removing distractions, and revising regularly make effort more productive than long, unfocused hours. Wasting time through procrastination or constant distractions breeds stress and regret, while consistent, deliberate action builds competence and confidence. Time also governs natural rhythms—sleep, work, and play—which must be balanced to sustain energy and creativity. Respecting other people’s time—by being punctual and prepared—fosters trust and opens opportunities. Ultimately, valuing time means making choices that align daily actions with long-term aims: learning instead of delaying, helping instead of hesitating, and practicing instead of postponing. By treating time as a limited treasure and stewarding it thoughtfully, we shape a future rich in achievement, meaning, and peace.

b Time is an irreplaceable resource; plan and act with discipline, avoid procrastination, and prioritize tasks for lasting success and fulfillment daily.

c 1. Make a daily timetable: Allocate specific slots for study, rest, and revision to convert vague intentions into concrete actions.
2. Use focused intervals (e.g., [math]25[/math]-[math]50[/math] minutes) and short breaks: This maintains concentration and prevents burnout.
3. Eliminate key distractions: Turn off unnecessary notifications and set a dedicated study space to protect your attention.
4. Prioritize tasks with a short-to-long list: Tackle high-impact tasks first so important work is done when energy is highest.

d 1. "Time and tide wait for no man." — Opportunities and natural cycles pass by; act promptly because delays can mean lost chances.
2. "Lost time is never found again." — Time wasted cannot be recovered, so using each moment wisely is essential to achieving goals.

Explanation:
The composition (a) is structured to introduce the central idea, explain its importance especially for students, offer practical habits, and conclude with the broader moral—matching the original task style but with fresh wording. The 20-word summary (b) distills the core message into a concise, memorable sentence suitable for headings or captions. The practical tips (c) reflect evidence-based productivity strategies: scheduling creates commitment, focused intervals (Pomodoro-like) boost efficiency, removing distractions protects cognitive resources, and prioritizing ensures impact-first effort. The use of [math]25[/math]-[math]50[/math] minutes is illustrative of common focused-session lengths; the [math] [/math] tags were added to follow the required formatting rule for any numeric/math areas. The sayings and explanations (d) connect traditional proverbs to actionable meaning—why they still matter today. Overall, the responses aim to be instructive, concise, and directly applicable for students seeking to improve time usage.

Question #138 (ID: 578733)
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Type: CQ | Book: English 2nd Paper | Years:  | Boards:  | Tags: 
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Question:
You are preparing educational material about fruits grown across Bangladesh for a school project. The material should include a short composition and specific notes on seasonal fruits, export origins, and ways to promote fruit-eating habits among children.

Options:
a. Write a composition of about 200 words titled "Seasonal Fruits of Bangladesh."
b. Name five fruits commonly available in summer in Bangladesh and give one-sentence health benefit for each.
c. Mention two fruits that are widely exported from Bangladesh, and state the regions they are especially famous for.
d. Suggest three practical ways to encourage children to eat more fruits (briefly explain each).


Correct Answer: 

Solution:
a Seasonal Fruits of Bangladesh: Bangladesh is blessed with a warm, humid climate and fertile soils that support a rich variety of fruits throughout the year. Summer brings an abundance of mangoes, jackfruits, litchis, pineapples, and watermelons, each enjoyed for their juicy sweetness and cooling qualities. In cooler months, guavas, oranges, and custard apples become plentiful, offering refreshing flavors and vitamin C to strengthen immunity. Some fruits, like bananas, coconuts, and papayas, are available year-round and form staples in many households. Fruit trees not only supply nutritious food but also sustain livelihoods: many small farmers cultivate orchards and sell produce in local markets or export premium varieties. Regionally, Rajshahi’s mangoes and Barisal’s guavas have earned reputations for quality, attracting buyers from across the country and abroad. Including fruits in daily meals provides essential vitamins, minerals, fiber, and natural energy while helping prevent diseases and supporting healthy growth in children. To preserve this natural bounty, communities must protect orchards, adopt better storage and transport methods, and encourage young people to learn fruit farming. In this way, the fruits of Bangladesh remain a source of health, beauty, and economic strength for generations to come.

b Five common summer fruits and one-sentence benefits:
1. Mango — Rich in vitamin A and C, mangoes boost immunity and support eye health.
2. Jackfruit — High in carbohydrates and fiber, jackfruit provides sustained energy and aids digestion.
3. Litchi — Contains vitamin C and antioxidants that help protect cells and support skin health.
4. Watermelon — With high water content and electrolytes, watermelon hydrates and cools the body.
5. Pineapple — Contains bromelain and vitamin C which help digestion and reduce inflammation.

c Two exported fruits and their famous regions:
1. Mango — Rajshahi region is renowned for varieties like Langra and Himsagar, widely traded and exported.
2. Guava — Barisal and surrounding districts produce flavorful guavas that are sent to domestic and foreign markets.
These fruits contribute to farmers' incomes and foreign exchange through seasonal trade and export.

d Three ways to encourage children to eat more fruits:
1. Make fruits attractive: Cut fruits into fun shapes or arrange colorful fruit platters so children find them visually appealing.
2. Involve children in preparation: Let them wash, peel, or assemble fruit salads to increase their interest and ownership.
3. Combine fruits with favorite foods: Add fruits to breakfasts, yogurts, or desserts (e.g., mango yogurt or banana pancakes) to create familiar, tasty options.
Each method increases familiarity, appeal, and habit formation, making children more likely to choose fruits regularly.

Explanation:
The composition (a) summarizes seasonal availability, nutritional value, regional importance, and socio-economic roles of fruits in Bangladesh, using accessible language for students. For (b), each listed summer fruit is paired with a key health benefit grounded in common nutritional properties: mangoes (vitamins A and C), jackfruit (energy and fiber), litchi (vitamin C and antioxidants), watermelon (hydration), and pineapple (bromelain and vitamin C). In (c), Rajshahi mangoes and Barisal guavas are cited because they are historically and commercially notable regions associated with high-quality produce and export activity; mentioning regions links local geography to economic impact. For (d), the suggested strategies draw on behavioral principles—visual appeal, active involvement, and combining with familiar flavors—to create positive experiences that increase children's willingness to eat fruits. Together, the answers aim to be practical, educational, and suitable for a school project that highlights both health and economic perspectives on Bangladesh’s fruits.

Question #139 (ID: 578736)
